Definition of Jaiz in God's Actions
Sanusi defines 'jaiz' for God's actions and gives its proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means for God's actions to be permissible (jaiz) and why they are not rationally obligatory or impossible.

Core Idea
God's actions are all permissible; nothing is rationally obligatory or impossible for Him.

Sanusi defines the term 'jaiz' as applied to God's actions, clarifying that God is not obligated to do what is best (al-aslah). He provides a proof that if any action were obligatory or impossible, it would contradict the nature of possible things. Let's explore his concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage aloud or silently. Then read the literal English translation to grasp the meaning.
Notice the compact style Sanusi uses.
2
Identify the definition
Find the sentence that defines 'al-jaiz'. Notice that it includes both action and leaving, and that nothing is obligatory upon God.
He mentions 'la yajibu' (not obligatory) and 'la yastahilu' (not impossible).
3
Follow the proof
The author argues that if an action were obligatory or impossible, then a possible thing would become necessary or impossible, which is false. Understand why this is a contradiction.
This proof shows that all actions are within the realm of possibility for God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يجوز في حقّهِ تعالى فعل كل ممكن أو تركه وأما الجائز فهو كل فعل من أفعاله تعالى لا يجب عليه منه شيء ولا مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح وإلا لما وقعت محنة دنيا ولا أخرى ولا تكليف بأمر ولا نهي يعني أن أفعاله تعالى كلها جائزة لا يجب منها عليه شيء عقلاً ولا يستحيل إذ لو وجب شيء منها عقلاً أو استحال عقلاً لانقلب الممكن واجباً أو مستحيلاً ولا يخفى بطلان ذلك قوله : ( كل فعل من أفعاله ) يعني وكذلك كل ترك من تروكه تعالى فإنه أيضاً جائز لا واجب ولا مستحيل وإنما استغنى بالفعل عن الترك لأنه مقابله والحكم على أحد المتقابلين بالجواز يستلزم مثله في مقابله
It is permissible with respect to God, exalted, to do every possible thing or to leave it. As for the permissible: it is every action from His actions, exalted; nothing from it is obligatory upon Him, nor is observing what is beneficial or most beneficial (obligatory). Otherwise, there would have occurred no trial in this world or the next, nor any command or prohibition. He means that all of His actions, exalted, are permissible; nothing from them is obligatory upon Him rationally nor impossible. For if something from them were rationally obligatory or rationally impossible, the possible would turn into necessary or impossible, and the falsity of that is not hidden. His statement: 'every action from His actions' means: and likewise every leaving from His leavings, exalted, for it is also permissible, not obligatory nor impossible. He sufficed with 'action' over 'leaving' because it is its counterpart, and the ruling on one of the two counterparts with permissibility implies the same for its counterpart.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage defines what is meant by the permissible (jaiz) in relation to God's actions. The author begins by stating that it is permissible for God to do every possible thing or to leave it. Then he defines the permissible as every action of God that is not obligatory upon Him, and He is not required to observe what is beneficial or most beneficial. If that were obligatory, then there would be no trials or commands and prohibitions in this world and the next. The author explains that all of God's actions are permissible: they are neither rationally obligatory nor rationally impossible. The proof is that if any action were obligatory or impossible, the possible would become necessary or impossible, which is absurd. Finally, the author clarifies that 'every action' includes 'every leaving' as well, because leaving is the counterpart of action, and the ruling of permissibility on one implies the same on the other. The reason he mentioned only action is that it suffices due to the relation of counterparts.

Key Terms
الجائز
فعل
ترك
واجب
مستحيل
صلاح
أصلح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God is obligated to do what is best (al-aslah) for His creatures.
Correction: The passage denies this, stating that no action is obligatory upon God, including observing what is beneficial or most beneficial.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would happen if an action of God were rationally obligatory or impossible?
A) It would still be permissible.
B) The possible would become necessary or impossible.
C) It would contradict divine wisdom.
D) God would cease to be necessary.

Key Takeaway
God's actions are all permissible; nothing is rationally obligatory or impossible for Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God is not obligated to do what is most beneficial for you, what is the true basis of your hope in His mercy? Does your heart truly rely on His sheer generosity, not on any perceived 'right' to His favor?
?
When you face a trial, do you ever feel that God 'should not' have allowed it because it contradicts your sense of fairness? How does this lesson's truth that God is bound by no rational obligation challenge that feeling? What would it take for you to surrender that expectation?
?
You now know that God's actions are all permissible — He is free. Are you willing to accept that His freedom might mean He does things that seem unfair to you? Can you worship a God who owes you nothing, yet gives you everything? If not, what must change in your heart?

The Permissible in God's Acts: Definition and Scope
Sanusi defines al-ja'iz and explains its application to God's acts and omissions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what al-ja'iz means in the context of God's actions and why all of God's acts are permissible without rational obligation or impossibility.

Core Idea
All of God's actions are permissible — none are obligatory or impossible.

Sanusi begins by defining a key term: al-ja'iz (the permissible). In Sunni theology, this applies to all of God's acts, meaning He is not rationally bound to do or refrain from anything. This lesson establishes that foundation and clarifies that the permissibility covers both actions and omissions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a compressed style.
2
Identify the two denials
The definition denies both obligation and impossibility. Find the phrases that express these denials.
The phrase 'لا يجب عليه منه شيء' denies obligation, and 'لا يستحيلُ' denies impossibility.
3
Understand the logical argument
Sanusi argues that if any act were obligatory or impossible, then the possible would become either necessary or impossible, which is absurd. Grasp this modal reasoning.
This shows the foundation of kalam reasoning.
4
Note the inclusion of omissions
Sanusi explicitly states that omissions are also covered by permissibility. See how he derives this from the counterpart relation.
This ensures the definition is comprehensive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الجائز (۱) : فهو كلُّ فعل مِنْ أفعاله تعالى ، لا يجب عليه منه شيء ، ولا مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح ، وإلا لَمَا وقعَتْ محنة دنيا ولا أخرى ، ولا تكليف بأمر ولا نهي
يعني : أنَّ أفعالَهُ تعالى كلَّها جائزة ، لا يجب منها عليه شيء عقلاً ولا يستحيلُ ؛ إذ لو وجبَ منها شيء عقلاً ، أو استحال عقلاً..
لانقلب الممكن واجباً أو مستحيلاً ، ولا يخفى بطلان ذلك
قوله : ( كلُّ فعلِ مِنْ أفعالِهِ ) يعني : وكذلكَ كلُّ تَرْكِ مِنْ تُرُوكِهِ تعالى ؛ فإنَّهُ أيضاً جائز ، لا واجب ولا مستحيل ، وإنَّما استغنى بالفعل عن التركِ ؛ لأنَّه مقابله ، والحكم على أحد المتقابلين بالجواز يستلزم مثله في مقابله
بطلان القول بمراعاة الصلاح والأصلح في حقه تعالى ] قوله : ( ولا مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح ) يعني : وكذلك لا يجب عليهِ تعالى أنْ يُوجِدَ لخلقِهِ ما هو صلاح لهم أو هو أصلحُ ، والمراد : ما ضدُّه صالح إلا أَنَّهُ : ما ضده فساد (۱) بالصلاح وبالأصلحِ دونه ، وهذا مِنْ عطفِ الخاص على العام ؛ لأنَّ الصلاح والأصلحَ داخلان في عموم قولِهِ : ( كلُّ فعلٍ مِنْ أفعالِهِ تعالى ) ، وإِنَّما نبَّهَ عليهِ بالخصوص ؛ إشارةً لمذهب المعتزلة الذينَ أوجبوا ذلك عقلاً في حقه تعالى ; فالبغداديون منهم : أوجبوا على الله تعالى ما هو أصلح لعبادِهِ في الدين والدنيا ، والبصريون منهم : أوجبوا عليه تعالى ما هو الأصلح لهم في الدين فقط
As for the permissible (al-ja'iz): it is every action from His actions, may He be exalted, nothing of it is obligatory upon Him, nor the observance of what is proper (salāh) nor most proper (aslah). Otherwise, there would be no trial in this world or the next, nor any command or prohibition. Meaning: that all His actions, may He be exalted, are permissible; nothing of them is rationally obligatory upon Him nor impossible; because if something of them were rationally obligatory or rationally impossible, then the possible would become obligatory or impossible, and the falseness of that is not hidden. His saying: 'every action from His actions' means: and likewise every omission from His omissions, may He be exalted; for it too is permissible, not obligatory nor impossible. He sufficed with the action over the omission because it is its counterpart, and the judgment of permissibility on one of the two counterparts entails the same for its counterpart. Refutation of the saying regarding the observance of what is proper and most proper in His right, may He be exalted: His saying: 'nor the observance of what is proper nor most proper' means: and likewise He, may He be exalted, is not obligated to bring into existence for His creation what is proper for them or what is most proper. The intended meaning: what its opposite is proper, except that it is: what its opposite is corruption, by 'proper' and 'most proper' lesser than it. And this is a specification of the general from the general, because proper and most proper are included in the generality of His saying: 'every action from His actions, may He be exalted.' He only alerted to it specifically as an indication of the doctrine of the Mu'tazila who obligated that rationally in His right, may He be exalted. Among them, the Baghdādīs obligated upon God, may He be exalted, what is most proper for His servants in religion and this world; and the Baṣrans obligated upon Him, may He be exalted, what is most proper for them in religion only.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage defines al-ja'iz (the permissible) in the context of God's actions and refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine that God must do what is most beneficial (al-salāh wa al-aslah). It begins by stating the definition: all of God's actions are permissible, none are obligatory or impossible. The phrase 'nor observance of what is proper nor most proper' denies any obligation on God to consider the welfare of creation. The author explains that if such obligation existed, there would be no trial or obligation in the world, which contradicts observable reality. The commentary then clarifies that the term 'every action' includes omissions, since the permissibility of an action implies the permissibility of its opposite. The second section explicitly refutes the Mu'tazila who claimed God must do what is most beneficial. Two factions are mentioned: the Baghdādīs obligate the most proper in both religion and world, while the Baṣrans limit it to religion. The passage affirms that all of God's acts are permissible without any rational necessity.

Key Terms
الجائز
الوجوب
الاستحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God is obligated to act in certain ways, such as doing what is good or beneficial.
Correction: All of God's actions are permissible; nothing is rationally obligatory or impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what does al-ja'iz mean for God's actions?
A) Actions that are obligatory for God.
B) Actions that are impossible for God.
C) Actions that are neither obligatory nor impossible.
D) Actions that are recommended for God.

Key Takeaway
All of God's actions and omissions are equally permissible, with no rational obligation or impossibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God is not obligated to do anything for you, how does that change the way you pray — your sense of entitlement, your patience, your desperation?
?
Think of a recent trial you experienced. Did you, even slightly, feel that God 'should have' spared you? How does this lesson reframe that feeling, and what would it take to surrender that expectation completely?
?
Knowing that even God's omission (withholding blessings, delaying relief) is purely permissible and not a failure of obligation, what does that demand from your heart when you face unanswered prayers? Will you bow to His absolute freedom without bitterness?

Refuting the Obligation of What is Proper and Most Proper
Sanusi refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine of al-salāh wa al-aslah and distinguishes their two schools.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni refutation of the Mu'tazila claim that God must do what is most beneficial for creation.

Core Idea
God is not obligated to create the best possible world for His creatures.

Having established that all of God's acts are permissible, Sanusi directly refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine that God must observe what is proper (salāh) and most proper (aslah) for His creation. This lesson explains that refutation and the division between the Baghdadi and Basran Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Note that it begins with 'بطلان القول' indicating a refutation.
This is a direct polemic against the Mu'tazila.
2
Understand the denial
Sanusi denies that God is obligated to create what is proper or most proper. Find the phrase that states this.
The phrase 'لا يجب عليه تعالى أن يوجِدَ لخلقِهِ ما هو صلاح لهم أو هو أصلحُ' is the denial.
3
Appreciate the two Mu'tazila factions
Sanusi mentions two groups: the Baghdādīs (obligate most proper in both religion and world) and the Baṣrans (obligate only in religion). Note the difference.
This shows internal diversity among the Mu'tazila.
4
Connect to the general principle
The doctrine of proper and most proper is a specific case of the general rule that all acts are permissible. See how Sanusi makes this connection.
He says it is included in the generality of 'every action'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الجائز (۱) : فهو كلُّ فعل مِنْ أفعاله تعالى ، لا يجب عليه منه شيء ، ولا مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح ، وإلا لَمَا وقعَتْ محنة دنيا ولا أخرى ، ولا تكليف بأمر ولا نهي
يعني : أنَّ أفعالَهُ تعالى كلَّها جائزة ، لا يجب منها عليه شيء عقلاً ولا يستحيلُ ؛ إذ لو وجبَ منها شيء عقلاً ، أو استحال عقلاً..
لانقلب الممكن واجباً أو مستحيلاً ، ولا يخفى بطلان ذلك
قوله : ( كلُّ فعلِ مِنْ أفعالِهِ ) يعني : وكذلكَ كلُّ تَرْكِ مِنْ تُرُوكِهِ تعالى ؛ فإنَّهُ أيضاً جائز ، لا واجب ولا مستحيل ، وإنَّما استغنى بالفعل عن التركِ ؛ لأنَّه مقابله ، والحكم على أحد المتقابلين بالجواز يستلزم مثله في مقابله
بطلان القول بمراعاة الصلاح والأصلح في حقه تعالى ] قوله : ( ولا مراعاة صلاح ولا أصلح ) يعني : وكذلك لا يجب عليهِ تعالى أنْ يُوجِدَ لخلقِهِ ما هو صلاح لهم أو هو أصلحُ ، والمراد : ما ضدُّه صالح إلا أَنَّهُ : ما ضده فساد (۱) بالصلاح وبالأصلحِ دونه ، وهذا مِنْ عطفِ الخاص على العام ؛ لأنَّ الصلاح والأصلحَ داخلان في عموم قولِهِ : ( كلُّ فعلٍ مِنْ أفعالِهِ تعالى ) ، وإِنَّما نبَّهَ عليهِ بالخصوص ؛ إشارةً لمذهب المعتزلة الذينَ أوجبوا ذلك عقلاً في حقه تعالى ; فالبغداديون منهم : أوجبوا على الله تعالى ما هو أصلح لعبادِهِ في الدين والدنيا ، والبصريون منهم : أوجبوا عليه تعالى ما هو الأصلح لهم في الدين فقط
As for the permissible (al-ja'iz): it is every action from His actions, may He be exalted, nothing of it is obligatory upon Him, nor the observance of what is proper (salāh) nor most proper (aslah). Otherwise, there would be no trial in this world or the next, nor any command or prohibition. Meaning: that all His actions, may He be exalted, are permissible; nothing of them is rationally obligatory upon Him nor impossible; because if something of them were rationally obligatory or rationally impossible, then the possible would become obligatory or impossible, and the falseness of that is not hidden. His saying: 'every action from His actions' means: and likewise every omission from His omissions, may He be exalted; for it too is permissible, not obligatory nor impossible. He sufficed with the action over the omission because it is its counterpart, and the judgment of permissibility on one of the two counterparts entails the same for its counterpart. Refutation of the saying regarding the observance of what is proper and most proper in His right, may He be exalted: His saying: 'nor the observance of what is proper nor most proper' means: and likewise He, may He be exalted, is not obligated to bring into existence for His creation what is proper for them or what is most proper. The intended meaning: what its opposite is proper, except that it is: what its opposite is corruption, by 'proper' and 'most proper' lesser than it. And this is a specification of the general from the general, because proper and most proper are included in the generality of His saying: 'every action from His actions, may He be exalted.' He only alerted to it specifically as an indication of the doctrine of the Mu'tazila who obligated that rationally in His right, may He be exalted. Among them, the Baghdādīs obligated upon God, may He be exalted, what is most proper for His servants in religion and this world; and the Baṣrans obligated upon Him, may He be exalted, what is most proper for them in religion only.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage defines al-ja'iz (the permissible) in the context of God's actions and refutes the Mu'tazila doctrine that God must do what is most beneficial (al-salāh wa al-aslah). It begins by stating the definition: all of God's actions are permissible, none are obligatory or impossible. The phrase 'nor observance of what is proper nor most proper' denies any obligation on God to consider the welfare of creation. The author explains that if such obligation existed, there would be no trial or obligation in the world, which contradicts observable reality. The commentary then clarifies that the term 'every action' includes omissions, since the permissibility of an action implies the permissibility of its opposite. The second section explicitly refutes the Mu'tazila who claimed God must do what is most beneficial. Two factions are mentioned: the Baghdādīs obligate the most proper in both religion and world, while the Baṣrans limit it to religion. The passage affirms that all of God's acts are permissible without any rational necessity.

Misconception: God is obligated to create the best possible world for His creatures.
Correction: No, God is not obligated; He does whatever He wills, and all His acts are permissible.

Checkpoint

B) Baghdādīs obligate most proper in both religion and world; Baṣrans only in religion.
C) Baghdādīs deny obligation; Baṣrans affirm it.

Key Takeaway
God's acts are not constrained by what is most beneficial for creation; He is free and wise.

?
When you make du'a, do you approach with the expectation that Allah should answer in a particular way because you 'deserve' it? How does this passage expose that expectation as a form of hidden entitlement, and what must change in the way you supplicate to reflect true servitude?

Rational Proof for the Possibility of Seeing Allah
Sanusi presents the classic kalam argument that existence is the common cause making vision possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical steps of the rational proof that existence is the common cause making vision of Allah possible.

Core Idea
Existence, not temporality, is the sole cause enabling vision.

This lesson unpacks a famous rational proof from classical kalam. By carefully tracing the argument's steps, you will see how scholars reasoned that the vision of Allah is not only possible but logically grounded in the nature of existence itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in.
2
Identify the logical steps
Notice how the proof moves from observation to conclusion. List each step in your own words.
The proof has about 6–7 steps.
3
Examine the key terms
Identify terms like wujūd, ḥudūth, jawhar, ‘araḍ. Understand their meanings in this context.
Refer to the key_terms list.
4
Understand why temporality is rejected
Focus on the reasoning that eliminates ḥudūth as a cause. Why does non-existence disqualify it?
This is a crucial move in the argument.
5
Connect to the broader theological point
See how the proof supports the possibility of seeing Allah, a key doctrine in Sunni theology.
The conclusion is that since existence is shared, the Necessary Being can be seen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأَمَّا ما استُدِلَّ بهِ على جواز الرؤيةِ مِنَ الدليل العقلي المشهور : أنَّ الرؤية لما صح تعلَّقها بالجواهر وبالأعراض ، وكانَتْ صحة الرؤية أمراً يتحقَّقُ عند الوجودِ وينتفي عند العدم ؛ لزم أن تكون لها علَّةٌ لامتناعِ الترجيح بلا مُرجّح ، وأن تكون تلك العلَّةُ مشتركة بينَ الجوهر والعرض ؛ لامتناع تعليل الحكم الواحد بعلتين ، وهي إمَّا الوجود وإما الحدوث ؛ إذْ لا ثالث لهما يصلح للعليَّةِ ، والحدوث أيضاً غير صالح للعليَّةِ ؛ لأنَّهُ عبارةٌ عن مسبوقية الوجودِ بالعدم ، وهو اعتباري محض ، أو عن الوجودِ بعد العدم ، ولا مدخل للعدم في صحَةِ الرؤيةِ ، فتعيَّن الوجود ، وهو ممَّا يشترك فيه الواجب والجائز .
As for what is used as evidence for the permissibility of the vision from the famous rational proof: that since vision is validly attached to substances and accidents, and the validity of vision is a matter that is realized upon existence and negated upon non-existence, it must have a cause, because of the impossibility of preponderance without a preponderator, and that cause must be common between substance and accident, because of the impossibility of explaining one ruling by two causes. That cause is either existence or temporality (ḥudūth), since there is no third that is suitable for causality. And temporality is also not suitable for causality, because it is an expression of existence being preceded by non-existence, which is a pure mental consideration, or it is existence after non-existence, and non-existence has no entry in the validity of vision. Therefore existence is determined, and it is something that the Necessary and the contingent share.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents the famous rational proof for the possibility of the vision of Allah (ru'yah). The argument proceeds step by step: First, we observe that vision can attach to both substances (jawāhir) and accidents (a‘rāḍ). Second, the validity of vision is realized when the object exists and negated when it ceases to exist. This indicates that the validity of vision depends on a cause, because a state that varies with existence and non-existence cannot be without a cause (otherwise it would be arbitrary). Third, that cause must be a single, common cause shared by both substances and accidents; it cannot be two different causes, because a single effect cannot have two distinct causes. Fourth, the only possible causes are existence (wujūd) or temporality (ḥudūth), as nothing else is suitable for causality. Fifth, temporality is ruled out: it is either the mental concept of being preceded by non-existence (a mere consideration) or existence after non-existence—but non-existence has no role in making vision valid. Therefore, existence alone remains as the cause. Sixth, existence is common to both the Necessary Being (wājib) and contingent beings (jā’iz). Hence, since contingent beings can be seen, and existence is the cause, the Necessary Being can also be seen. The argument is a classic kalam demonstration that the vision of Allah is rationally possible, not impossible.

Key Terms
رؤية
علَّة
وجود
حدوث
جوهر
عرض
واجب
جائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think temporality (ḥudūth) could serve as the cause of vision because all visible things are created.
Correction: Temporality is rejected because it involves non-existence, which plays no role in making vision valid. Only existence itself is the cause.

Checkpoint
What does the rational proof identify as the common cause that makes vision possible?
A) Temporality (ḥudūth)
B) Existence (wujūd)
C) Substance (jawhar)
D) Accident (‘araḍ)

Key Takeaway
The rational proof establishes that existence alone is the cause of vision, making the vision of Allah logically possible since He exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that Allah exists, yet do you live as if His existence is the most real thing in your life? If existence itself makes vision possible, why do you still feel distant from Him? What would change if you truly internalized that the One who exists desires to be seen by you in the hereafter?
?
The proof rules out temporality because non-existence plays no part in vision. Reflect: Are you more attached to things that come and go (temporal) than to the Eternal? If Allah's existence is the cause of vision, why do you invest so much in fleeting things and so little in preparing for that vision?
?
This argument makes the vision of Allah logically possible. Yet do you long for that vision with the same urgency you long for worldly sights? If not, what does that reveal about the state of your yaqeen (certainty)? Will you today make a concrete intention to increase your desire to see your Lord?

Weakening of the Rational Proof and Its Clarification
Al-Taftazani reports al-Juwayni's clarification of 'cause' in the vision proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the controversy around the rational proof for vision of Allah and how al-Juwayni's clarification of 'cause' resolves most objections.

Core Idea
Objections collapse when 'cause' means 'valid object' not 'efficient cause'.

Sanusi has presented a rational proof for the permissibility of seeing Allah. However, many later scholars, especially Imam al-Razi, raised numerous objections that seemed to weaken the proof. In this brief passage, al-Taftazani reports that al-Juwayni already clarified a crucial point: the 'cause' in the proof refers to the attribute that makes something a valid object of vision, not the efficient cause that produces vision. Understanding this distinction dissolves most criticisms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic original and its literal translation to grasp the historical debate described. Notice the names: al-Razi (the objector), al-Taftazani (the reporter), and al-Juwayni (the clarifier).
This is a short passage, so read slowly.
2
Identify the key distinction
The core of the lesson is al-Juwayni's distinction between 'cause' as efficient cause vs. 'cause' as valid object. Understand what each means in this context.
The efficient cause would be the agent that makes vision happen; the valid object is the attribute that qualifies something as seeable.
3
Apply the distinction
Consider how al-Razi's objections might be based on the wrong understanding of 'cause'. If 'cause' means valid object, the objections no longer apply. Think of an example: existence is the attribute that allows something to be seen, not that causes the sight.
This shows how a single terminological clarification can rescue an argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فضعفه كثير من المتأخرين، لا سيما الإمام الفخر، واعترضه بوجوه كثيرة. قال التفتازاني: ( يندفع أكثرها بما دل عليه كلام إمام الحرمين من أن المراد بالعلة هنا: ما يصح متعلقاً للرؤية، لا المؤثر في الصحة على ما فهمه الأكثرون ) .
Many of the later scholars weakened it, especially Imam al-Fakhr [al-Razi], and he objected to it in many ways. al-Taftazani said: 'Most of them are repelled by what the words of Imam al-Haramayn [al-Juwayni] indicate, that what is meant by “cause” here is that which is a valid object of vision, not the efficient cause in validity, as most understood.'
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage reports the critical reception of the rational proof presented earlier. Many later scholars (al-muta'akhkhirūn), most notably Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, weakened the argument by raising numerous objections. However, al-Taftazani (a later commentator) cites Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni's clarification that resolves most of the objections: the 'cause' (ʿilla) in the proof does not mean the efficient cause that makes vision happen (mu'aththir fī al-ṣiḥḥa), but rather it refers to that which is a valid object of vision (mutaʿallaq li al-ru'ya). In other words, the argument shows that existence is the attribute that qualifies something as visible, not that it causes the act of vision. Once this distinction is understood, most objections no longer apply.

Key Terms
المتأخرون
الإمام الفخر
التفتازاني
إمام الحرمين
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Students often think that 'cause' (ʿilla) in the proof means efficient cause.
Correction: Al-Juwayni clarifies that it means the attribute that makes something a valid object of vision, not the efficient cause of vision.

Checkpoint

A) The efficient cause of vision
B) The object of vision (mutaʿallaq li al-ru'ya)
C) The essence of the seer
D) The absence of a veil

Key Takeaway
The rational proof for vision is not weakened by objections if we understand 'cause' correctly, as the attribute of existence that makes something visible, not as the agent that produces vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Al-Juwayni's insight—that a single clarified term can dissolve many objections—reveals how precise theology protects belief. What false assumption about God might you be holding simply because you have not examined the exact meaning of a term you use in prayer or creed? Will you investigate it today?
?
Most of al-Razi's objections were repelled, but not all. Does this humility—that some questions remain unanswered—trouble you? Or can you trust that the scholars who preserved these proofs examined carefully? When you face unresolved theological puzzles, do you turn to deeper study or to impatience?

Taftazani's Defense of the Proof for Vision of God
Sanusi reports Taftazani's defense of the proof for ru'ya against al-Razi's objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani defends the proof for the vision of God by clarifying the meaning of 'cause' in the argument.

Core Idea
The 'cause' in the proof for vision is existence as an object of vision, not an efficient cause.

Sanusi reports that Taftazani responded to al-Razi's objections by clarifying a key term. Understanding this clarification is essential to preserve the proof for ru'ya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage

Notice the shift from the problem to the solution.
2
Identify the main claim
Pinpoint that Taftazani says most objections are repelled by distinguishing two meanings of 'illa (cause).
3
Understand the distinction
The two meanings: (1) what qualifies something as a valid object of vision, and (2) the efficient cause that brings about vision. The proof only uses the first.
This is the key insight that defuses al-Razi's objections.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this distinction matters for the overall proof that God can be seen in the afterlife.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer. The author notes that a certain proof for the possibility of seeing God was weakened by many later scholars, most notably Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, who raised numerous objections against it. Then al-Taftazani answers that most of these objections are deflected by a clarification from Imam al-Haramayn: the term 'cause' (illa) in the proof refers only to what makes an entity a valid object of vision (i.e., its existence), not to the efficient cause that brings about the vision itself. Refuting the Ray Theory of Vision
Sanusi enumerates two logical consequences of the ray theory and refutes them by direct observation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two empirical arguments that disprove the Mu'tazili claim that vision occurs by rays emanating from the eye.

Core Idea
If vision depended on rays, we would see very little, very late—but we see vast scenes instantly.

Sanusi presents a swift refutation of the Mu'tazili theory of vision. Instead of engaging in complex philosophical argument, he points to two simple observations that anyone can verify: the capacity and immediacy of sight. These observations show the theory is logically and empirically untenable. Read on to see how he exposes the absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional consequences
Sanusi states two necessary consequences if the ray theory were true: limited scope and delayed perception. Find them in the text.
Look for the phrases 'لازم' and 'لوجب'.
2
Contrast with direct observation
He then says 'both matters are invalid by direct observation'. Reflect on how everyday experience disproves each consequence.
Notice he uses the word 'المعاينة' (direct observation) as evidence.
3
Understand the conclusion
After showing the contradictions, Sanusi concludes the theory is a delusion and leads to corruption of intellect and religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأدلته كثيرة، يطول تتبعها، وقد ذكرنا كثيراً منها في العقيدة الكبرى وشرحها (١)، ويكفي هنا في رد ذلك عليهم باختصار: أن الرؤية لو كانت بانبعاث الأشعة كما يزعمون للزم ألا يرى الإنسان إلا قدر حدقته؛ إذ لا تسع من الأشعة التي ترى بها عندهم أكثر منها، ولوجب أن تتأخر رؤية الرائي لما بعد عنه بعد فتح عينه أزمنة بقدر ما تصل الأشعة إلى المرئي وتتصل به، ويختلف ذلك باختلاف البعد. وكلا الأمرين باطل بالمعاينة؛ فإن الإنسان يرى الأشياء البعيدة جداً بنفس فتح عينه من غير تأخر أصلاً، ويرى دفعة في لحظة واحدة أكثر من ذلك أضعافاً مضاعفة لا حصر لها، فضلاً عن حدقته التي ظرف الأشعة التي لا يرى إلا ما اتصلت به عندهم (٢). فقد ظهر لك من هذا هوس هذه المقالة، وفساد العقل والدين الذي ابتلي به هؤلاء القوم، ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم.
(١) شرح العقيدة الكبرى (ص ٥٠٨)
(٢) ما ذكره المصنف هنا هو من باب زيادة دليل إلى دليل، فعدم التسليم به لا يضر في ثبوت أصل الرؤية، ولا سيما أن العقل يجوزها، والشرع يرجحها. واعلم: أن المشبهة شاركوا المعتزلة في كونه سبحانه وتعالى لا يرى إلا بتلك الشروط، ولذا أثبتوا الجسمية له جل شأنه وعز، فكانوا على التحقيق ممن ينكر الرؤية، ولذا قال العلامة ابن التلمساني في ( شرح معالم أصول ) (ص ٣٨١): ( وأهل السنة قالوا: يصح أن يرى مع تنزهه عن الكيفية، فهم - يعني: الكرامية والحشوية من الحنابلة - مخالفون لهم معنى وإن ساعدوهم لفظاً )
Its proofs are many, lengthy to follow, and we have mentioned many of them in al-ʿAqīda al-Kubrā and its commentary (1). Sufficient here in refuting them briefly is: that if vision were by the emission of rays as they claim, then necessarily a person would not see except to the extent of his pupil; since it cannot contain more of the rays by which he sees according to them, and it would be necessary that the seeing of the seer of what is distant from him be delayed after opening his eye by periods of time according to the arrival of the rays to the visible object and their connection to it, and that would differ according to distance. Both matters are invalid by direct observation; for a person sees very distant objects at the very moment of opening his eye without any delay, and sees at once in a single moment many times more than that, countless multiples, let alone his pupil which is the container of the rays that he sees only what is connected to them according to them (2). Thus you have seen from this the delusion of this claim and the corruption of intellect and religion with which these people have been afflicted. And there is no power and no strength except with Allah, the Exalted, the Magnificent. (1) Sharḥ al-ʿAqīda al-Kubrā (p. 508) (2) What the author mentioned here is of the type of adding proof to proof; non-acceptance of it does not harm the establishment of the principle of vision, especially since intellect permits it and revelation prefers it. And know: that the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) joined the Muʿtazila in that He, glorified and exalted, is not seen except under those conditions, and therefore they affirmed corporeality for Him, and thus in reality they are among those who deny vision. Hence the scholar Ibn al-Talmisānī said in (Sharḥ Maʿālim al-Uṣūl, p. 381): 'The Ahl al-Sunna said: it is valid [for Him] to be seen with His being transcendent of modality; they — meaning the Karāmiyya and the Hashwiyya among the Ḥanābila — oppose them in meaning even if they agree with them in wording.'
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazili claim that vision occurs by the emission of rays (shu'ā'āt) from the eye. The author presents two practical arguments based on observation to demonstrate the absurdity of this view. First, if vision depended on rays, a person could only see as much as the pupil can contain of those rays, but in fact we see vast scenes with a single glance, far exceeding the size of the pupil. Second, if rays must travel from the eye to the object, there would be a time delay for distant objects, but we see them instantly when we open our eyes. Both contradictions show the theory is false. The author then declares this claim a sign of intellectual and religious corruption. The appended footnote clarifies that the refutation is an additional argument; the core proof for the possibility of seeing God rests on rational permissibility and scriptural preference. It also notes that the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) ironically agree with the Mu'tazila in denying true vision of God, because they impose physical conditions, and thus they are effectively deniers. A quote from Ibn al-Talmisānī distinguishes the Ahl al-Sunna position: God can be seen without modality, unlike the corporealists who are only verbally affirming vision.

Key Terms
الرؤية
الأشعة
الحدقة
المعاينة
بانبعاث الأشعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ray theory refutation is the only evidence for the possibility of seeing God.
Correction: It is an additional argument; the core evidence is rational permissibility and scriptural preference, as stated in the footnote.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which two empirical observations refute the ray theory of vision?
A) The eye cannot see distant objects, and vision requires light.
B) We see objects instantly without delay, and we see many things at once.
C) The pupil changes size, and rays bend.
D) Vision depends on air, and objects must be illuminated.

Key Takeaway
The ray theory of vision is false because it contradicts our immediate experience of seeing vast scenes and distant objects without delay.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that a false belief about physical vision can corrupt intellect and religion. What false beliefs about Allah or His attributes have you accepted without questioning? Are you willing to examine them against clear evidence from the Qur'an and reason?
?
When you open your eyes and see the world instantly, do you recognize Allah's power in creating vision as it truly is? What would change in your daily awareness if you constantly reminded yourself that this is a direct gift from Allah, not a product of a flawed theory?
?
The author calls the ray theory 'هوس هذه المقالة' (the delusion of this claim). Are there any delusions about your own faith that you cling to out of habit or peer pressure? How can you seek the truth with the same decisiveness as Sanusi?

Who Really Denies Seeing Allah?
Sanusi clarifies the role of the refutation and exposes the sectarian trap of the Mushabbiha.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Mushabbiha (anthropomorphists) are considered to actually deny the vision of Allah despite their verbal affirmation.

Core Idea
Those who impose physical conditions on seeing God end up denying it altogether.

In an important footnote, Sanusi clarifies that the refutation of the ray theory is not the only basis for affirming the vision of Allah. More critically, he warns that some groups who claim to affirm vision are themselves deniers. This lesson explains that irony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditions mentioned
The footnote says that the Mushabbiha agree with the Mu'tazila that Allah cannot be seen except under certain conditions (like being in a direction). Find that claim.
These conditions imply corporeality.
2
Understand the consequence
Because they impose physical conditions, they end up affirming a body, which contradicts true transcendence. Thus in reality they deny vision.
The text says 'they are among those who deny vision'.
3
Remember the Ibn al-Talmisani quote
The quote distinguishes Ahl al-Sunna's position (seeing without modality) from the anthropomorphists' position (seeing with modality).
The quote concludes they are only verbally agreeing.

Multiplicity of Sight and Its Objects
Sanusi argues for the finitude of perceptions and refutes infinite regress in sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the multiplicity of sight is tied to the finitude of its objects, and why this refutes the possibility of infinite regress in perception.

Core Idea
Our sight multiplies with its objects, and both are finite — so no infinite regress.

Sanusi presents a key rational argument about the nature of sight. He draws an analogy with knowledge to show that perceptions multiply according to their objects. Then he introduces the concept of an 'impediment' to explain why not all objects are perceived. Finally, he refutes a potential infinite regress by noting that both objects and their perceptions are finite.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a compressed rational style here.
2
Understand the analogy with knowledge
Sanusi compares sight to knowledge: both multiply according to their objects. This establishes the principle that perceptions are relative to the multiplicity of what is perceived.
This analogy is important for the overall argument.
3
Identify the concept of impediment
If a perception does not occur for a visible object, it is because an impediment (manīʿ) prevents it. Note that impediments also multiply according to the number of unseen objects.
The impediment is an opposing meaning that blocks perception.
4
Follow the refutation of infinite regress
Sanusi concludes that both perceptions and impediments are finite because they are related to finite existents. Thus, no infinite regress occurs in the eye.
This refutes the objection that multiple perceptions would imply infinite entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بصرنا يتعدَّدُ بحَسَبِ تعدُّدِ مُتعلَّقِهِ ، فلكلِّ مَرْنِيٌّ بصر يخصُّهُ ، كما أَنَّ ذلك حكم العلم ؛ فإنَّهُ يتعدَّدُ في حقنا بتعددِ المعلوماتِ ، وكلُّ ما يجوز أنْ يُدرَكَ بالبصرِ ؛ فإذا لم يقم بالمحلَّ إدراك يتعلَّقُ بهِ لزمَ أَنْ يقوم بالمحلّ معنى يُضادُّ إدراكه ، وهو المعبر عنه في اصطلاح الموحدين بالمانع ، وتتعدد تلك الموانع بحسَبِ تعدُّدِ تلكَ الموجودات التي لم تر . ولا يلزمُ مِنْ تعدُّدِ تلكَ الإدراكات وتعددِ موانعِها قيام ما لا يتناهى عدده بالعينِ ؛ لأنَّ إدراك البصرِ إِنَّما يتعلق بالموجود ، والموجودات متناهيةٌ ، فإدراكاتها وموانعها التي هي أضدادها متناهية .
Our sight multiplies according to the multiplicity of its object, so for every seen object there is a sight specific to it, just as that is the case with knowledge; for it multiplies in us by the multiplicity of information. And everything that may be perceived by sight: if there does not occur in the locus a perception related to it, then there must occur in the locus a meaning that opposes its perception, which is termed in the terminology of the monotheists the impediment. And those impediments multiply according to the multiplicity of those existing things that are not seen. And it does not follow from the multiplicity of those perceptions and the multiplicity of their impediments the subsistence of what is infinite in number in the eye; because the perception of sight is only related to the existent, and existents are finite, so their perceptions and their impediments which are their opposites are finite.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a rational argument about the nature of sight and its objects. It begins by stating that our sight multiplies according to the multiplicity of its objects, analogous to knowledge which multiplies by the multiplicity of known things. Then it introduces the concept of an impediment (al-mani'): if a perception does not occur for a visible object, there must be an opposing meaning that prevents it. These impediments also multiply according to the unseen existents. The conclusion is that since existents are finite, both perceptions and their impediments are finite, so there is no infinite regress in the eye.

Key Terms
بصر
إدراك
مانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Multiple perceptions would require infinite entities in the eye.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that since the objects of sight are finite, both perceptions and their impediments are finite, so no infinite regress.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does the multiplicity of sight not entail infinite perceptions in the eye?
A) Because the eye can only perceive a finite number of objects at once.
B) Because perceptions are not actual entities.
C) Because the objects of sight are finite.
D) Because impediments prevent infinite perceptions.

Key Takeaway
The multiplicity of sight does not lead to infinite regress because the objects of sight are finite, and perceptions and their impediments are likewise finite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Sanusi argues that every unseen existent has a specific impediment preventing its perception. What unseen spiritual reality (like your own death or Allah's omnipotence) are you currently blocking from your inner sight, and what worldly attachment is that impediment? Will you remove it today?
?
Sight and knowledge both multiply with their objects. Your knowledge of Allah multiplies with every sign you ponder. Is your ma'rifa of Him growing each day, or have you stopped at a finite set of facts? What single sign of Allah will you study deeply today to multiply your knowledge?
?
The passage concludes that because existents are finite, perceptions and impediments are finite. Your days are finite. Are you filling your perception with finite distractions while blocking vision of the hereafter? What is the first impediment you must remove from your heart's eye?

Abu Ishaq's Rational Proof of Allah's Truthfulness
Sanusi presents Abu Ishaq's rational proof for impossibility of lying by Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Ishaq's rational proof that lying is impossible for Allah, derived from the necessary connection between perfect knowledge and truthful self-expression.

Core Idea
A being with infinite knowledge cannot lie because lying requires ignorance.

Sanusi presents a powerful rational argument from Abu Ishaq that establishes Allah's truthfulness as a necessary consequence of His perfect knowledge. The proof does not rely on prophetic reports; it is purely rational. By following the logical steps, we see that speech must match knowledge, and since Allah's knowledge is complete, lying—which requires ignorance—is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Contextualize the argument
Notice that Sanusi begins with a conditional remark: if we have already negated lying by rational proof (without prophetic report), then there is no problem. This sets the stage for the purely rational proof.
Sanusi emphasizes that this proof does not rely on revelation.
2
Understand Abu Ishaq's premise
Abu Ishaq states that every knower necessarily has speech within himself that corresponds to his known object. This speech is the reality of a truthful report.
3
Apply to Allah's perfect knowledge
Since Allah knows all things as they truly are, His speech must be in accordance with that knowledge. Thus, His speech is necessarily truthful.
This step connects the general premise to the specific case of Allah.
4
Conclude the impossibility of lying
Lying is defined as reporting something contrary to reality. This would require ignorance of the true state of affairs. Ignorance is impossible for a being whose knowledge encompasses the infinite. Therefore, lying is impossible for Allah.
The proof hinges on the incompatibility of ignorance with infinite knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما لو تم لنا نفي الكذب عنه تعالى بالدليل العقلي مِنْ غيرِ إخبار النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ . . فلا إشكال حينَئِذٍ (١) . استحالة الكذب على الله سبحانه وتعالى ] وقد استدل الأستاذ أبو إسحاق على استحالة الكذب على الله تعالى عقلاً : بأنْ قالَ : كلُّ عالم بأمرٍ يلزمُ أنْ يكونَ في ذاتِهِ حديث يطابق معلومه ، وهذا هو حقيقة الخبر الصادقِ . والله تعالى عالم بالأمورِ كلَّها على ما هي عليهِ ، فيكونُ كلامه جلَّ وعزَّ على وَفْقِ ذلكَ ، فاستحال الكذبُ عليه إذاً ؛ وهو الخبر عنِ الشيء بخلافِ ما هو عليه ؛ لأنَّهُ لا يكونُ في حقِّهِ إلا عن جهل ، وذلك في حقٌّ مَنْ عمَّ علمُهُ ما لا يتناهى . . محال
As for if we have negated lying from Him, Exalted, by rational proof without the report of the Prophet, peace and blessings be upon him, then there is no problem at that time. [Impossibility of lying for Allah, Glorified and Exalted] And the teacher Abu Ishaq has argued for the impossibility of lying for Allah, Exalted, rationally, by saying: Every knower of a thing necessarily has within himself speech that corresponds to his known object; this is the reality of truthful report. And Allah, Exalted, knows all things as they are, so His speech, Majestic and Honored, is in accordance with that; thus lying is impossible for Him, and it is reporting something contrary to what it is, because it does not occur in His case except from ignorance, and that is impossible in the case of One Whose knowledge encompasses what is infinite.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for the impossibility of lying for Allah, introduced by a conditional remark: if we have already negated lying by rational proof without relying on prophetic reports, then no problem. The proof itself is attributed to Abu Ishaq: every knower necessarily has within his essence a speech that corresponds to his knowledge—this is the definition of truthful report. Since Allah knows all things as they truly are, His speech must be in accordance with that knowledge, which is truthfulness. Lying, which is reporting something contrary to reality, would require ignorance. Ignorance is impossible for One whose knowledge encompasses the infinite. Therefore, lying is impossible for Allah. The argument hinges on the necessary intrinsic connection between knowledge and truthful self-expression in a simple being.

Key Terms
العلم
الخبر الصادق
الكذب
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Lying requires ignorance only in humans, not in divine beings.
Correction: The proof shows that lying, by definition, is reporting contrary to reality, which can only arise from ignorance. For a being with infinite knowledge, ignorance is impossible, so lying is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Abu Ishaq's proof, what is the definition of a truthful report?
A) Any statement that is factually correct.
B) Speech within the knower's essence that corresponds to his known object.
C) Speech that is confirmed by external evidence.
D) A report that is not contradictory.

Key Takeaway
Lying is impossible for Allah because it would require ignorance, which cannot coexist with His infinite knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does this proof affect your confidence in Allah's promises and warnings? Are there areas where you doubt His truthfulness? What would it take for you to fully trust that every word from Him is true?
?
Since lying is impossible for Allah, why do you sometimes distrust His plan for your life? Examine your heart: what fear or ignorance drives that distrust?
?
If Allah cannot lie, then every verse in the Qur'an is absolutely true. Which verse do you find hardest to fully accept in your daily life? What would it cost you to believe it completely and act accordingly?

Objection and Answer: Human Lying vs. Divine Simplicity
Sanusi records an objection to the proof and provides the Ash'ari answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the objection about lying preserves the proof that Allah's knowledge necessitates truthfulness.

Core Idea
Divine simplicity demands truthfulness; human composition allows lying despite knowledge.

Having established that Allah's knowledge entails truthfulness, Sanusi now addresses an objection. The objector argues that a knowledgeable person can lie intentionally, so truth does not necessarily follow knowledge. The Ash'ari answer hinges on a crucial distinction: in composite humans, knowledge resides in a different locus than speech, but Allah, who is simple, cannot have such separation. This lesson unpacks that objection and its powerful rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the flow of the argument.
Pay attention to the objector's evidence and the response's key distinction.
2
Analyze the objection
Identify the premise the objector challenges: that lying only comes from ignorance. Note the counterexample of a knowing person lying intentionally.
3
Understand the Ash'ari distinction
Notice how the answer distinguishes between the locus of knowledge and the physical organ of speech (the tongue). The knowing part cannot lie, but the speaking part can.
This relies on human composition (tarkīb).
4
Apply to divine simplicity
Reflect on why this distinction does not apply to Allah. Since He is not composite, His knowledge and speech are from the same essence, so truthfulness is necessary.
5
Draw the conclusion
Conclude that the proof for Allah's truthfulness remains valid: the objection fails because it mistakenly assumes Allah is like composite beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتُرِضَ على هذهِ الحُجَّةِ : بأنَّا لا نُسلَّمُ أَنَّ الكذبَ لا يكون إلا عن جهل ؛ بدليل أنَّ العالِمَ منا بأمرٍ قد يخبر عنه بالكذب عمداً ، ولم يلزم مِنْ كذبهِ جهلُهُ ، فليسَ العلم إذاً ملزوماً للصدق ، ولا الكذب ملزوماً للجهل . وأجيب عنه : بأنَّ الجزء الذي قام به العلم منا يستحيل أن يخبر بالكذب ، وإنَّما الذي يخبرُ بالكذب منَّا غيرُه ؛ كعُضْوِ اللسان (۲) ، فقد قام العلم والصدقُ منا بمحل والكذب بمحل آخرَ لمَّا كانَتْ ذواتنا مُركَّبَةٌ ، والإله جلَّ وعلا يستحيلُ عليه التركيب حتى يقوم العلمُ والصدقُ بمحل والكذب بمحل آخر .
And an objection was raised against this proof: that we do not accept that lying only occurs from ignorance; evidenced by the fact that a knower among us may intentionally give a lying report about a matter, and his knowledge does not necessarily entail his ignorance. So knowledge is not necessarily connected to truthfulness, nor is lying necessarily connected to ignorance. And it was answered: that the part of us in which knowledge resides is impossible to make a lying report; rather, that which makes a lying report from us is something else, like the organ of the tongue. Thus, knowledge and truthfulness reside in one locus in us, and lying in another locus, because our essences are composite. But the Divine, Exalted and Glorified, is impossible for composition, such that knowledge and truthfulness could be in one locus and lying in another.
Source pages p. 459

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to Abu Ishaq's proof and its answer. The objector claims that lying is not necessarily caused by ignorance; a knowing person can lie intentionally. Therefore, knowledge does not guarantee truthfulness, nor does lying imply ignorance. This would undermine the proof that Allah's knowledge necessitates truthfulness. The answer distinguishes between the locus of knowledge and the physical organ of speech in composite beings. The part of a human where knowledge subsists (the self or intellect) cannot lie; the lying originates from other parts like the tongue. Since humans are compound, it is possible for knowledge to be in one part and lying in another. However, Allah is not composite; He is simple. Thus, if His knowledge subsists in His essence, His speech cannot come from a different part, so it must be truthful. Therefore, the objection fails because it relies on human composition, which does not apply to Allah.

Key Terms
التركيب (al-tarkīb)
محل (maḥall)
اللسان (al-lisān)
الجزء (al-juzʾ)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection disproves the proof entirely, showing that knowledge does not necessitate truthfulness.
Correction: The objection only applies to composite beings like humans; for non-composite beings like Allah, knowledge and speech are from the same essence, so truthfulness is necessary.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why can a human lie despite having knowledge?
A) Because knowledge does not always lead to truth.
B) Because lying can be intentional even if one knows the truth.
C) Because the knowing part is separate from the part that produces speech.
D) Because ignorance is not always the cause of lying.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari answer preserves the link between knowledge and truthfulness for non-composite beings like Allah by distinguishing loci in composite beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector says a knower can intentionally lie. When have you done that—spoken against what you knew to be true? How does the Ash'ari answer expose that such lying comes from a different 'part' of you than your intellect? What would it take for your tongue to truly serve your knowledge, so that your speech becomes more truthful?
?
If Allah's simplicity makes His speech necessarily truthful, then your own composition gives you the capacity to separate knowledge from speech. In what area of your life are you most 'composite'—saying one thing while knowing another? What is one specific step you will take today to bring your tongue into alignment with your heart?
?
The answer shows that your intellect itself cannot lie—it is the tongue that betrays it. Have you ever reflected on the fact that when you lie, you are actually betraying your own deepest self? How can you strengthen your resolve to let your tongue be a reflection of your intellect, as Allah's speech is a reflection of His essence?
